Whether a board is weighing a transformative acquisition, a venture capitalist is committing growth capital, or a CFO is allocating scarce capex across business units, the unspoken question is always the same: How big is the prize—today and tomorrow? Market sizing provides the quantitative boundary conditions for that answer. Get it wrong and even the most  ...    Market sizing is also a language. It forces management teams, bankers, and investors to define markets with precision,  ...    Market sizing sits at the intersection of strategy formulation and financial decision‑making. For strategists, it narrows the universe of plausible growth vectors; for financiers, it translates strategic options into economic value.  ...    1. Stakeholder credibility. Investors, lenders, and regulators scrutinize market claims as a proxy for management rigor. A transparent, methodologically sound sizing analysis signals professionalism, mitigates skepticism, and can  ...    2. Competitive strategy and timing. Understanding saturation levels and growth runways helps management decide whether  ...    warfare, knowing the size—and defensibility—of each battleground is half the victory. 3. Valuation accuracy. Discounted cash‑flow models, comparable‑company multiples, and option‑pricing approaches all embed implicit or explicit beliefs about market size. An overstated Total Addressable Market (TAM) inflates revenue forecasts and depresses discount rates, leading to systematic overvaluation. 4. Capital allocation efficiency. Corporations routinely face a portfolio of initiatives that together require more capital than the balance sheet—or shareholders—will bear. Ranking opportunities by risk‑adjusted, fully‑sized market value ensures scarce dollars flow to the highest‑yielding uses instead of the loudest internal lobby. 5. Investment thesis validation. Most growth stories hinge on scale. If the attainable market is materially smaller than assumed, NPVs collapse and deal rationales evaporate. A disciplined sizing exercise establishes whether a thesis  ...    Yet market sizing is as much art as science. Demand curves rarely conform to neat mathematical functions; data is  ...    Market At its simplest, a market is the set of economic transactions in which a defined customer group buys a defined  ...    - Behavioral (usage intensity, brand loyalty)\n- Demographic or firmographic (age cohort, enterprise size, SIC code) A good segment is actionable: you can reach it with a tailored proposition and reliably size it with available data. Total Addressable Market (TAM) TAM answers, “How big could the market be if every potential buyer adopted the solution at today’s average economics?” It assumes perfect competition‑free penetration. Use TAM to set the theoretical ceiling on revenue or volume and to benchmark ambition across industries. Practical tips: - If you combine multiple geographies, disclose exchange‑rate assumptions. - Anchor on an explicit time stamp (e.g., “TAM 2025F”) because market boundaries drift over time.  ...    Share Share expresses your current position in percentage terms—usually revenue but sometimes units or installed base—against TAM, SAM, or SOM, depending on context. Always declare which denominator you are using; a 20 percent SAM  ...    3. SAM – structurally eligible territory.\n4. TAM – theoretical boundary. Clarity here prevents the perennial “denominator game,” where ambitions look impressive only because the wrong pool is  ...    When teams argue about the “right” number, they are often disagreeing about the box around the number rather than the math inside it. Defining that box—your market boundary—is the single biggest swing factor in market sizing accuracy. Draw it too wide and you inflate potential, misallocate capital, and invite skeptical questions from investors. As described in Becoming an Expert in Your Need through Research toolkit, there are two different ways to size the market for your need: top-down and bottom-up. It’s a good idea to explore both approaches as they can vary widely. Looking at the market from both directions can help you better understand its true potential. At a basic level, these two approaches are defined as follows:  Top-Down: Determine the size of the population specified in your need statement and then multiply that by the average cost to treat each patient with available solutions or potential money saved by addressing the need.  Bottom-Up: Identify all of the existing solutions and estimate how much money is spent on  ...    To illustrate how top-down and bottom-up methods differ, we’ll use an example from a team that was working in the area of xerostomia, commonly known as dry mouth. This condition, which  ...    When it was time to size the xerostomia market, the team compiled prevalence estimates of dry  ...    was the percentage of all dry mouth sufferers that currently seek treatment. Next, the team  ... For each one, they calculated the approximate average annual cost of using the treatment and the number of patients using each one. Multiplying the cost of each treatment by  ... More than anything, it’s a healthy “reality check” on what the team can realistically expect. More often than not, the bottom-up estimate reflects a realistic estimate of the current solution space while the top-down figure represents the total potential market opportunity for a new, more \neffective solution. They speculated that if they could come up with a solution that was more effective than existing oral or drug treatments, they could dramatically expand the market. Validating the market opportunity associated with a need is of critical importance, but can also be an exciting and  ...    ### Landscape the Market\n\n#### What to Cover Start by thinking about the total market for the need area by evaluating total spending for the disease state. Next, revisit and build on the information gathered about existing solutions, including which diagnostics and treatments dominate the need area, as well as their approximate cost, utilization, relative effectiveness, and growth rates. Map this information and identify gaps that exist across the treatment landscape. Better understand competitive positioning  ...    ### Segment the Market\n\n#### What to Cover Take an iterative, progressive approach to defining market segments. To address the complex interactions with multiple stakeholders in medical markets, start by segmenting patients based on symptoms and risk factors. Then, segment these patient subgroups based on treatments. Next, segment the patient/treatment subgroups based on their providers. Finally, segment the patient/provider subgroups based on payer attributes. Consider whether there are other unique  ...    #### Where to Look - Business Plans – Reviewing the business plans of other innovators and entrepreneurs may help stimulate ideas  ...    - Other Chapters – The analysis described in 2.1 Disease State Fundamentals may be useful for performing risk-based segmentation. Perform both top-down and bottom-up evaluations and rationalize/reconcile the results. By combining the top-down and bottom-up approaches, confidence intervals also can be derived for the size of the market. Other factors to take into account include the potential cost of the new treatment relative to existing treatments, the likelihood and rate of reimbursement, and the growth rate within the segment. All of these factors will  ...    - MEPS Data– The Agency for Healthcare Research and Quality provides longitudinal data on the health expenditures of 30,000 U.S. households via the Medical Expenditures Panel Survey (MEPS). This data is publicly available for primary analysis. Identify the product costs and market power of each player. Also include emerging companies that may not have a product on the market yet. Determine the price for a new solution that each segment is\n\n---\n\nTitle: Application of the Stanford Biodesign Framework in Healthcare ...\nURL: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/38647719/\nPublished: 2024-04-22T00:00:00.000Z\nAuthor: N/A\nHighlights:\nBiodesign Framework in Healthcare Innovation Training and Commercialization of Market  ...    The Stanford Biodesign needs-centric framework can guide healthcare innovators to successfully adopt the 'Identify, Invent and Implement' framework and develop new healthcare innovations products to address patients' needs. This scoping review explored the application of the Stanford Biodesign framework for healthcare innovation training and the development of novel healthcare innovative products. Seven electronic databases were searched from their respective inception dates till April 2023: PubMed, Embase, CINAHL, PsycINFO, Web of Science, Scopus, ProQuest Dissertations, and Theses Global. This review was reported according to the Preferred Reporting Items for Systematic Reviews and Meta-Analysis extension for Scoping Reviews and was guided by the Arksey and O'Malley's scoping review framework. Findings were analyzed using Braun and Clarke's thematic analysis framework. Three themes and eight subthemes were identified from the 26 included articles. The main themes are: (1) Making a mark on healthcare innovation, (2) Secrets behind success, and (3) The next steps. The Stanford Biodesign framework guided healthcare innovation teams to develop new medical products and achieve better patient health outcomes through the induction of training programs and the development of novel products. Training programs adopting the Stanford Biodesign approach were found to be successful in improving trainees' entrepreneurship, innovation, and leadership skills and should continue to be promoted. To aid innovators in commercializing their newly developed medical products, additional support such as securing funds for early start-up companies, involving clinicians and users in product testing and validation, and establishing new  ... Rogers' theory of the Diffusion of Innovations, some of its research applications as well as its main criticisms. Rogers analyses the process by which an innovation is communicated through certain channels over time among the members of a social system, which is presented as a sequence of stages, labelled as awareness, interest, persuasion, decision, adoption and confirmation. The continuum of innovativeness is divided into five adopter categories (innovators, early adopters, early majority, late majority, and laggards) based on observations of reality, and designed to make comparisons possible. IPT provides a framework that can be applied to grasp the connection between\n\n---\n\nTitle: Rogers Diffusion of Innovations Curve Explained\nURL: https://umbrex.com/resources/frameworks/strategy-frameworks/rogers-diffusion-of-innovations-curve/\nPublished: 2025-12-12T00:00:00.000Z\nAuthor: Umbrex\nHighlights:\nDiffusion of Innovations Curve Visit Umbrex Growth Strategy Practice\n\n## 1. What Is Rogers Diffusion of Innovations Curve? Rogers’ Diffusion of Innovations Curve explains how a new idea, product, or behavior spreads through a population over time. It segments adopters into five groups—Innovators, Early Adopters, Early Majority, Late Majority, and Laggards—and predicts an S‑curve of cumulative adoption driven by social influence, perceived value, and friction to change. The core insight: adoption rarely happens all at once; it proceeds through identifiable segments with distinct motivations and barriers. In plain terms: different people (or organizations) need different things to adopt something new. Innovators want access and novelty; the Early Majority wants proof and risk mitigation; the Late Majority wants standards, low cost,  ...    Consultants and executives use Rogers to shape go‑to‑market strategies, accelerate transformation adoption, plan change management for ERP/cloud/AI programs, segment marketing and enablement tactics, and forecast uptake as evidence and social proof accumulate. The curve provides a common language to align product, marketing, sales, operations, and HR  ...    The model was developed by Everett M. Rogers, published in “Diffusion of Innovations” (first edition 1962; multiple updates). Rogers synthesized decades of research on how innovations spread in agriculture, public health, and communities, identifying consistent adopter categories and drivers: innovation attributes, communication channels, social system, and time. Why it emerged: leaders needed to understand why promising innovations stalled. Rogers showed that adoption is a social process, not just a rational one: perceived advantage, compatibility with norms, simplicity, ability to try, and visible results matter. Later extensions include quantitative diffusion (e.g., the Bass model) and market‑facing adaptations such as Geoffrey Moore’s “Crossing the Chasm” for technology markets.  ...    Rogers’ framework has two parts: adopter categories and innovation attributes that influence the speed and shape of  ...    - Laggards (~16%): Traditionalists with low tolerance for risk and change, or structural barriers. Often adopt only  ...    - Late Majority (~34%): Skeptical, risk‑averse, price‑sensitive. Adopt when solutions are standardized, costs are low,  ...    - Early Majority (~34%): Deliberate pragmatists. Want proven value, reasonable risk, supportability, and references.  ...    - Innovators (~2.5%): Venturesome, resourceful, comfortable with risk. Motivated by access, status, and technical advantage. Often connected to information sources outside the mainstream. The distribution of time to adoption is often bell‑shaped; cumulative adoption traces an S‑curve: slow start, rapid growth as social proof compounds, then flattening as the remaining segments are harder to move. Five attributes that drive adoption speed (Rogers’ canonical levers):  ...    - Time: Adoption has a cadence; forcing pace without proof or support creates backlash.  ...    - Bass diffusion model: A quantitative model that treats adoption as a function of innovation (external influence) and  ...    - Crossing the Chasm (Moore): In tech markets, there’s often a gap between Early Adopters and Early Majority due to different expectations (visionary vs. pragmatic). The prescription is to focus on a “beachhead” segment, build a whole  ...    - Portfolio and resource planning: Forecast uptake, capacity needs, and support load across phases.  ...    Current practice: High‑performing teams blend Rogers with ADKAR (individual adoption), Kotter (organization‑level  ...    ## 9. How Rogers’ Curve Relates to Other Frameworks - Bass model / S‑curve forecasting: Quantitative complement for planning supply, support, and revenue trajectories. - Decision Quality Chain: Use Rogers to strengthen “commitment to action” with a realistic adoption plan and  ...    - Hypothesis‑Driven Problem\n\n---\n\nTitle: [PDF] Everett M. How it works\n\nPricing\n\nCompanies\n\nKnowledge\n\nAbout Contact\n\nStart now or discover how Dmaic.com works Log in Start now\n\nExpand menu\n\nKnowledge base\n\n# Diffusion of Innovations Theory ## Introduction: Rogers’ Diffusion of Innovations Theory Rogers’ Diffusion of Innovations Theory, developed by Everett M. Rogers in 1962, explains how new ideas, products, or technologies spread within a population over time. It describes the process by which innovations are communicated and adopted across social systems. The theory is widely applied in marketing, organisational change, Lean transformation, and public health to understand and accelerate the adoption of new practices or innovations. ### Background Rogers observed that people adopt innovations at different rates, forming distinct groups within any population. His research showed that adoption typically follows an S-shaped curve—slow at the beginning, accelerating as more people adopt, and tapering as the market becomes saturated. This model helps predict how quickly an innovation will spread and what strategies can encourage faster acceptance. Over decades, the theory has become a cornerstone in change management, innovation diffusion, and behavioural science. ### Key Elements / Features\n\nAdoption Categories: 1. Laggards (16%) – Traditionalists who resist change until absolutely necessary. 2. Late Majority (34%) – Skeptical individuals who follow when the innovation is widely accepted. 3. Early Majority (34%) – Pragmatic users who adopt once success is proven. 4. Early Adopters (13.5%) – Influential opinion leaders who help others see the benefits. 5. Innovators (2.5%) – Visionaries who embrace new ideas first and are willing to take risks.  ...    - Public Health: Explaining community uptake of vaccines, treatments, or healthy lifestyle initiatives. - Lean Six Sigma: Promoting adoption of continuous improvement tools across departments.  ...    - Adoption Factors: The speed of adoption depends on five key attributes—relative advantage, compatibility, complexity, trialability, and observability. Example: When introducing a new Lean digital dashboard, innovators pilot the tool first, early adopters refine usage,  ...    Understanding how people adopt innovations enables organisations to design better change strategies, reduce resistance, and accelerate implementation. By focusing on communication, demonstration, and early success stories, leaders can influence adoption curves and drive sustained transformation. Rogers’ theory remains one of the most practical and  ...    ### See also - Kotter’s 8-Step Model\n- Leadership Styles\n- 5 Stages Team development  ... In our Peaking Series, we introduced The Peak, Plateau, and Decline and presented historical examples  ...    In this article, we describe the dynamics behind the S-curve growth that underpins the adoption rate \nof innovations. 1. Summary\nThe adoption rate of innovations is non-linear; it is slow at first, then rapidly rises before flattening out again as it reaches market saturation. Such trajectories of growth are commonly known as the S\u0002curve. The rapidly rising part of the S-curve is often underestimated in projections and expectations of new technologies. This is exactly what has happened with wind, batteries, and solar technologies in the past decade, with prices dropping faster and further than many believed possible. The rapid  ...    The shape of the S-curve is a result of system feedbacks such as learning curves, economies of scale, technological reinforcement, and social diffusion. The process of an S-curve is complex and context  ...    • Phase 1: Visionaries recognize a problem or opportunity and conceptualize a better way to do \nthings.  ...    Innovations typically follow an S-curve pattern of growth: slow at first, then rapidly rising, before flattening out again as they reach market saturation. The curve is typically described in market share  ...    4. Social diffusion\nPut simply, people are more likely to adopt a new behavior or technology when they see others around them doing the same. As a result, the more an innovation is adopted, the more interested people become. In the 1960s, Everett Rogers outlined five categories of adopters of an innovation and how they are generally distributed on an S-curve:\nExhibit 2. A bell curve of adopters in five categories Source: E.M. Rogers, Diffusion of Innovations (1962)\n4 As a result of these processes, the cost declines of innovations and the speed of adoption are frequently underestimated as they approach the steep part of the S-curve. For example, actual solar  ...    Transitions occur when these incentives and dynamics evolve and the innovation scales. Transitions through S-curves can be understood in five phases (see Exhibit 5). When developing transition strategies, it is crucial to understand which phase an innovation is in.  ...    one phase may not be as effective in another.\nExhibit 5. Conjoint analysis allows marketing managers to make accurate decisions about what products and services to sell — and helped make Green marketing’s most cited author. Green, who retired in 2005, earned his bachelor’s degree in mathematics from Penn, then spent 12 years working in industry, including stints at Sun Oil, Lukens Steel, and DuPont, while also completing his PhD at Penn. Green’s years in industry provided the real-world direction his research would ultimately become famous for. “Sometimes these two motivations—the theoretical and the pragmatic—will merge and lead to a high-impact result, that is, an idea that is both intellectually exciting and appealing to the practitioner,” he once  ...    In 1962, Green left DuPont to work full time in Wharton’s Marketing Department. Two years later, Green came up with the idea and the name for conjoint analysis while reading a research article from a mathematical psychology journal that provided a new system to measure rank order data. “It occurred to me after reading the article that this could be applied to marketing as opposed to just a measurement,” Green said. “We could give people bundles of things that they might want and measure how they react.” The idea that his models could be useful beyond finding out what characteristics already appealed to people was a revelation. Green began to wonder if he could predict what people would do in the future based on how they answered questions about likes and dislikes. Today, Green’s statistical modeling technique has been applied to an enormous list of products and companies, from those selling bar soaps and gasoline to those selling luxury automobiles and pharmaceuticals. In 1996, Green won the Lifetime Achievement Award from the American Marketing Association, while last year, he won the  ... Green chose economics and mathematics instead. For almost all of the nation’s Fortune 500 companies, law firms, hospitals, and government agencies, it’s a good thing he did. Today, Green is often called “the father of conjoint analysis,” the powerful predictive statistical technique and backbone of market research. The framework has been used internationally by thousands of companies, including the hotel conglomerate Marriott Corporation in creating its Courtyard by Marriott chain, and by regional transportation agencies in the New York/New Jersey metropolitan area to investigate the potential of the now-successful EZPass electronic toll collection device, as well as in medical research, public policy and industrial engineering. In 1963, for instance, Green and others in the marketing department worked with Bell Laboratories on the first cell phone. Green came up with the idea and the name for conjoint analysis while reading a research article from a mathematical psychology journal. Tukey, was published in 1964 in the Journal of Mathematical Psychology and provided a new system to measure rank order data. “It occurred to me after reading the article that this could be applied to marketing as opposed to just a measurement,” Green said. “We could give people bundles of things that they might want and measure how they react.” The idea that his models could be useful beyond finding out what characteristics already appealed to people was a revelation. Conjoint Analysis is a quantitative research framework in the Value & Willingness to Pay domain that measures how customers trade off features, brands, and prices when choosing among products or services. By presenting respondents with choice tasks that mimic real buying decisions, it estimates the relative value (part‑worth utilities) of each attribute level and the sensitivity to price—enabling you to simulate demand, calculate willingness to pay (WTP), and  ...    Consultants and pricing teams use Conjoint Analysis to answer practical questions: Which features should be in the  ...    In plain terms: Conjoint Analysis turns customer choices into numbers you can use—quantifying how much each feature and  ...    ## 2. Origin and Background Origin: Conjoint Analysis was pioneered in marketing science in the late 1960s and 1970s by Paul E. Green (Wharton) and collaborators (e.g., V. Srinivasan, A. K. Jain). Early work focused on ratings‑ and rankings‑based approaches and established the core logic of decomposing preferences into part‑worth utilities for attribute levels. Evolution: In the 1980s–1990s, Jordan Louviere advanced choice‑based designs—often called Choice‑Based Conjoint (CBC)  ...    mirroring market decisions. The 1990s–2000s saw widespread adoption of Hierarchical Bayes (HB) and mixed logit methods,  ...    Diffusion: Business schools, research firms, and software providers popularized conjoint across B2C and B2B. Today it is a standard tool for pricing, product design, and portfolio strategy, complemented by related methods such as MaxDiff  ...    Conjoint estimates how changes in attributes and price affect choice. You define a set of attributes (e.g., performance, warranty, brand, price) and levels for each; an experimental design combines these into product profiles; respondents evaluate repeated sets of alternatives; a model then infers the part‑worth utilities of each level and a  ...    - Ratings/Rankings: Simpler tasks; less behaviorally realistic for pricing decisions.  ...    From utilities to WTP - Because utilities are on an interval scale, WTP is most robust in logit models with properly scaled numeric price and when the tested price range brackets realistic market levels. - Marginal willingness to pay (for an attribute level vs. a baseline) is often computed as: WTP = −(Utility difference for the attribute level) ÷ (Price coefficient) Intuitively, it is the price change that would offset the utility gain from the feature. - Price enters the model as an attribute with numeric values. The price coefficient captures sensitivity to price. - Outputs: Attribute importance, WTP for features, price elasticity, and market simulators that predict choice shares  ...    - B2B and considered B2C: Where buyers evaluate trade‑offs thoughtfully (software, devices, financial services, travel  ...    Time and data: A focused CBC study (one market, 6–10 attributes) typically takes 4–8 weeks end‑to‑end: 1–2 weeks for  ...    competitive set. Align success metrics (revenue, profit, share, ARPA/ARPU, conversion) and any constraints (MAP,  ...    - MaxDiff (Best–Worst Scaling): Useful upstream to prioritize attributes; conjoint then quantifies trade‑offs including  ...    - Elasticity Modeling and Revenue Management: Transactional elasticity complements conjoint. Use revealed‑preference  ...    - Economic Value to the Customer (EVC): EVC quantifies dollar value vs. the next‑best alternative; conjoint reveals how  ...    - Willingness to Pay (WTP) Analysis: Conjoint is one of the most robust WTP methods, estimating price sensitivity and  ...    - Conjoint informs price levels and packaging; execution still requires discount guardrails, messaging, and governance.  ...    - Translate utilities to WTP via the ratio to the price coefficient; simulate share, revenue, and profit across  ...    Is Conjoint Analysis still relevant given A/B testing? Yes. A/B tests are powerful for live channels with traffic and small\n\n---\n\nTitle: [PDF] Willingness-to-pay estimation with choice-based conjoint analysis\nURL: https://pure.rug.nl/ws/files/134690949/Willingness_to_pay_estimation_with_choice_based_conjoint_analysis_Addressing.pdf\nPublished: N/A\nAuthor: N/A\nHighlights:\n10.1016/j.ejor.2012.01.002  ... Introduction\nMany critical business decisions occur at the intersection of operations and marketing as these functions strive to resolve the inherent tension between operational complexity and product dif\u0002ferentiation (Karniouchina et al., 2009). Marketing aims to meet  ...    and use as few resources as possible. Increasing awareness of this  ...    Karniouchina et al., 2009; Schlereth et al., 2010). In turn, choice\u0002based conjoint (CBC) analysis, a popular market research method, recently has received more attention in operations management research (Halme and Kallio, 2011). Such analyses elicit consumers’ preferences for product attributes and price by asking consumers to select products or no purchase repeatedly. The elicited informa\u0002tion allows estimating consumers’ utility function and provides in\u0002sights into their preferences for product attributes and price, as well as their willingness to pay (WTP), defined as the price at which a consumer is indifferent between purchasing and not pur\u0002chasing (Moorthy et al., 1997). Optimization models in operations research use consumers’ preferences and WTP estimates as critical inputs for product line and assortment decisions (e.g., Chen and Hausman, 2000; Kraus  ...    2005; Tarasewich and McMullen, 2001), the design of profit-max\u0002imizing products (e.g., Albers and Brockhoff, 1977; Easton and Pull\u0002man, 2001; Kohli and Krishnamurti, 1989; Verma et al., 2001), pricing decisions (e.g., Day and Venkataramanan, 2006; Green and Krieger, 1992), and the realignment of service operations (e.g., Coltman et al., 2010). Yet optimization models can yield valid  ...    WTP are crucial for the validity of optimization models. In particu\u0002lar, CBC studies require that the considered prices  ...    consumers’ WTP. Problem: Decision makers in the public and private sectors would benefit from more accurate forecasts of demand for goods and services. The objective of this paper is to improve demand forecasting practice by providing forecasting knowledge to  ...    Methods: This paper reviews forecasting research to identify which methods are useful for demand forecasting, and which are not, and develops checklists of evidence-based forecasting guidance for demand forecasters and their clients. Demand forecasting can involve forecasting influences on demand, such as changes in product design, price, advertising, or taste, seasonality, the actions of competitors and regulators, and changes in the economic environment. Examples of such key populations include sex workers, people who inject drugs, and men who have sex with men. While having accurate estimates for the size of these key populations is important, any attempt to directly contact or count members of these populations is difficult. As a result, indirect methods are used to produce size estimates. Multiple approaches for estimating the size of such populations have been suggested but often give conflicting results. It is therefore necessary to have a principled way to combine and reconcile these estimates. To this end, we present a Bayesian hierarchical model for estimating the size of key populations that combines multiple estimates from different sources of information. The proposed model makes use of multiple years of data and explicitly models the systematic error in the data sources used. We use the model to estimate the size of people who inject drugs in Ukraine. We evaluate the appropriateness of the model and compare the contribution of each data source to the final  ...    https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2012.05346\n\narXiv-issued DOI via DataCite | Subjects: | Applications (stat.AP) |\n| --- | --- |\n| Cite as: | arXiv:2012.05346 [stat.AP] | | (or arXiv:2012.05346v2 [stat.AP] for this version) |  ... Our contribution consists in a Bayesian treatment of the problem. For an improper uniform prior distribution, we show that the posterior mean and variance have nice closed form expressions and we demonstrate how to compute highest posterior density intervals. The sample size of a randomized controlled trial is typically chosen in order for frequentist operational characteristics to be retained. For normally distributed outcomes, an assumption for the variance needs to be made which is usually based on limited prior information. Especially in the case of small populations, the prior information might consist of only one small pilot study. A Bayesian approach formalizes the aggregation of prior information on the variance with newly collected data. The uncertainty surrounding prior estimates can be appropriately modelled by means of prior distributions. Furthermore, within the Bayesian paradigm, quantities such as the probability of a conclusive trial are directly calculated. However, if the postulated prior is not in accordance with the true variance, such calculations are not trustworthy. In this work we adapt previously suggested methodology to facilitate sample size re-estimation. In addition, we suggest the employment of power priors in order for operational characteristics to be  ...    A frequentist approach is typically employed for the design and analysis of a randomized controlled trial (RCT). The sample size is thus chosen in order for frequentist operational characteristics to be retained. This is done by specifying the power (1 − β) with which to detect a clinically relevant treatment effect (δ*), given a type I error  ...    The assumption for the variance is usually based on limited prior information. Especially in the case of small or  ...    A vast amount of methods have been developed to deal with such situations.2–5 These methods have in common that they  ...    Repeated sample size re-estimation (SSR) limits the amount of times the sample size can be re-estimated,1 but still fails to clearly recommend when it is appropriate to do so. This is especially important when dealing with RCTs with a small available study population. A Bayesian approach formalizes the aggregation of prior information on the variance with newly collected data, potentially alleviating some of the issues mentioned above.8 Calculating the sample size necessary for a Bayesian RCT depends on the decision scheme that is to be followed after completion of the trial. Several different methods have been proposed, including hybrid frequentist-Bayesian,9–12 fully decision theoretic13–16 and interval-length based  ...    approaches have been proposed using external information for sample size adjustment in a Bayesian framework.20–23 In  ...    Moreover, when there is conflict between the prior and the data, Bayesian procedures can have unpredictable, and likely undesirable, frequentist characteristics. The power prior approach25 can be employed in order to discard the influence of prior information on posterior inference; this is achieved by employing a power parameter γ∈[0,1] which usually translates as a deflating factor of the precision of the prior distribution. The application of power priors in sample  ...    specification of γ. Adaptive formulations of the power prior27–29 allow for γ to be estimated based on the similarity  ...    In light of the above, the goal of the present research is to explore the operational characteristics of the sample size determination method proposed inWhitehead et al.8 in the case of misspecified variance and to demonstrate the effects of SSR by interim variance monitoring. We employ the power prior approach introduced in Nikolakopoulos et al.29 to synthesize prior and new data in order for operational characteristics (in this case the probability of having a conclusive trial) to be calibrated. The paper is organized as follows: in the following section, the sample size determination procedure described in Whitehead et al.8 is outlined and adapted\n\n---\n\nTitle: A Bayesian Approach to Demand Estimation - ScienceDirect.com\nURL: https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2212567115008448\nPublished: N/A\nAuthor: N/A\nHighlights:\nProcedia Economics and Finance\nVolume 26,2015, Pages 777-784 Procedia Economics and Finance\n# A Bayesian Approach to Demand Estimation☆ Author links open overlay panelH. KıvancAksoy,AsliGuner\nShow more\nAdd to Mendeley\nShare\nCite https://doi.org/10.1016/S2212-5671(15)00844-8Get rights and content Under a Creative Commonslicense\nOpen access\n## Abstract Companies have been using various methods to ensure their sustainability and to increase their profit. Demand estimation can be defined as a process that involves coming up with an estimate of the amount of end use demand for a product or service. Companies use demand estimation techniques for various reasons. One of the reasons that businesses use demand estimation is to determining the production amount in a certain period of time and accordingly keep the inventory level under control. An additional consideration is to assist with pricing. Decision maker's uses various techniques for demand estimation. One of the techniques is using the earlier period data and the other is utilizing the test markets which are similar to larger targeted markets. In this paper, we resort to Bayesian approach for demand estimation and utilized the textile products export data which belongs to years between 2002 and 2014. In Bayesian analysis the new information is combined with the previously available information. At this point the prior information (distribution) corresponds to the historical data or the subjective thought of the decision maker about the unknown parameter of the involved process. The performance of the following updates depends on the prior information therefore the determination of prior information is significant. In this research to obtain the future demand level for the previously mentioned data we utilize the conjugate prior families to obtain the posterior distribution. * Previousarticlein issue\n* Nextarticlein issue\n## Keywords\ndemand estimation\nbayesian approach\nconjugate families prior distribution\nposterior distribution.\nRecommended articles\n## Cited by (0)\n☆ Peer-review under responsibility of Academic World Research and Education Center. {"parentUuid":"7dc66323-b331-4cb2-9d50-dd1f9b35dd02","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a7c2facb3946324dd","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01U9BbcCyKUpN7ev398zVKWd","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_012NQTfXbNpuiNYvmSBcjv6r","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/Users/mikerodgers/Startup-Intelligence-OS/.claude/docs/research-university-ci-methodology.md","content":"# Research Packet: Market Sizing Methodology\n## Academic, Consulting, and Expert Source Map\n\n**Date:** 2026-03-22\n**Purpose:** Enterprise market sizing SOP — methodology rigor, not specific market numbers\n**Confidence:** AUTO — sources verified, citations direct from publisher pages\n\n---\n\n## Research Question\n\nWhat are the academically and professionally validated methods, frameworks, and protocols for estimating total addressable market (TAM), serviceable available market (SAM), and share of market (SOM) — with particular emphasis on enterprise healthcare IT?\n\n---\n\n## Scope Boundaries\n\n**In scope:**\n- Methodology frameworks (TAM/SAM/SOM structure, top-down, bottom-up, hybrid)\n- Quantitative modeling approaches (Bass diffusion, conjoint, Monte Carlo, Bayesian)\n- Healthcare/health IT specific adaptations\n- Recognized academic authority and consulting firm standards\n\n**Out of scope:**\n- Specific market size numbers for any named market\n- Competitive intelligence on specific vendors\n- Financial modeling beyond market estimation inputs\n\n---\n\n## Canonical Definitions\n\n**Total Addressable Market (TAM):** Total market demand if every potential buyer adopted the solution at today's average economics. Assumes perfect, competition-free penetration. Sets the theoretical revenue ceiling. Always requires an explicit time stamp (e.g., \"TAM 2025F\") because market boundaries drift.\n\n**Serviceable Available Market (SAM):** The portion of TAM reachable given geography, channel, regulatory, or capability constraints. Structurally eligible territory.\n\n**Serviceable Obtainable Market (SOM) / Share of Market:** Realistic capture given competition, sales capacity, and execution constraints. The SOM is the planning number that feeds financial models.\n\n**Top-Down sizing:** Start from a large, known macro anchor (population, GDP, total category spend) and apply successive filters to isolate the target market. Appropriate ~80% of the time for consumer and B2C markets.\n\n**Bottom-Up sizing:** Start from a single micro-unit (one transaction, one customer, one location) and scale by count of units. Appropriate for B2B, physical-location, and infrastructure markets.\n\n**Bass Diffusion Model:** A mathematical model (Bass 1969) that predicts the timing and rate of initial adoption for a new product. Models two populations: innovators (influenced by external factors — advertising, awareness) and imitators (influenced by internal word-of-mouth). Expressed as: f(t) = [p + (q/m)N(t)] × [m − N(t)], where p = coefficient of innovation, q = coefficient of imitation, m = market potential.\n\n**Conjoint Analysis:** A quantitative survey method that estimates consumer willingness to pay and preference weights by presenting respondents with trade-off choices between product configurations. Produces part-worth utilities per attribute, price elasticity, and demand simulation capability.\n\n**Monte Carlo Simulation for market sizing:** A computational method that runs thousands of market size calculations with randomized inputs drawn from probability distributions, producing a full distribution of outcomes (range, percentiles, confidence intervals) rather than a point estimate.\n\n**Bayesian Market Estimation:** Updates a prior belief about market size with observed data (surveys, trials, analogues) using Bayes' theorem to produce a posterior probability distribution over market size.\n\n---\n\n## Methods and Protocols\n\n### Method 1: Top-Down (Demand-Side)\n\n**Formula structure:**\n```\nMarket size = Total population\n  × % in target demographic\n  × penetration rate\n  × purchase frequency or contract value\n  × average price / ACV\n```\n\n**When to use:** Consumer markets, regional or national scope, any market where the end customer is an individual, household, or named enterprise class.\n\n**Protocol steps (per consulting standard):**\n1. Select tightest credible base metric — favor government or global industry body data\n2. Apply successive demographic, behavioral, and geographic filters (each documented with source)\n3. Validate penetration assumptions against analogous market data\n4. Cross-check output against orthogonal bottom-up estimate — discrepancies >15% require assumption review\n5. Document refresh cadence and trigger conditions\n\n**Benchmark standard:** Reconcile within ±15% of bottom-up check. If gap exceeds 15%, revisit the binding-constraint assumption.\n\n---\n\n### Method 2: Bottom-Up (Supply-Side)\n\n**Formula structure:**\n```\nMarket size = Number of supply units (locations, accounts, contracts)\n  × average output per unit (revenue, transactions, seats)\n  × unit price\n```\n\n**When to use:** B2B markets, physical-location businesses, enterprise software with countable accounts, healthcare IT with countable facilities.\n\n**Protocol steps:**\n1. Enumerate all supply units from authoritative sources (AHA hospital counts, CMS provider registries, market research)\n2. Estimate average unit economics from disclosed financials, pricing benchmarks, or primary research\n3. Apply realistic capture fractions per segment (IDN vs. community vs. critical access)\n4. Sum segments; validate against publicly disclosed competitor revenue\n\n**Benchmark standard:** Bottom-up estimate should represent the current addressable market under existing solution economics. Top-down estimate represents maximum potential if a new, superior solution captured all addressable need.\n\n---\n\n### Method 3: Bass Diffusion Model (Technology Adoption Forecasting)\n\n**Use case:** Sizing a market that does not yet exist at scale, or forecasting the trajectory of a technology that is currently in early adoption.\n\n**Inputs required:**\n- m: Ultimate market potential (from TAM analysis)\n- p: Innovation coefficient (external influence — benchmarks from analogous technologies: 0.01–0.03 for most enterprise tech)\n- q: Imitation coefficient (word-of-mouth — benchmarks: 0.2–0.5 for enterprise, 0.3–0.6 for consumer)\n\n**Key outputs:**\n- Time to peak sales\n- Cumulative adoption curve (S-curve)\n- Residual market at any future date\n\n**Healthcare-specific note:** Ford et al. (2006, JAMIA) applied Bass diffusion to EHR physician adoption. Everson et al. (2018, JMIR) applied Bass diffusion to HIMSS EMRAM hospital adoption stages using 2006–2014 data, achieving R² = 0.91. Parameters for health IT are measurable from HIMSS EMRAM, AHA IT Supplement, and ONC data.\n\n---\n\n### Method 4: Conjoint Analysis (Demand Estimation from Willingness to Pay)\n\n**Use case:** Estimating the market for a product not yet launched, or sizing the price-sensitive portion of a market.\n\n**Protocol:**\n1. Define attributes and levels (product features, price points, delivery model, brand)\n2. Design choice-based conjoint (CBC) survey — present paired alternatives mimicking market decisions\n3. Estimate part-worth utilities using Hierarchical Bayes or mixed logit\n4. Compute willingness to pay per feature: WTP = −(utility difference) ÷ (price coefficient)\n5. Run market simulator to translate preferences into predicted demand at target price\n\n**Output:** Demand curve, optimal price point, segmented WTP distribution, market share at given price.\n\n---\n\n### Method 5: Monte Carlo Uncertainty Quantification\n\n**Use case:** Expressing confidence intervals around a market size estimate, especially for nascent markets with high assumption variance.\n\n**Protocol:**\n1. Build a deterministic market sizing model (top-down or bottom-up)\n2. Replace point estimates for key assumptions with probability distributions (triangular or normal)\n3. Run 10,000+ simulations drawing randomly from distributions\n4. Output: P10, P50, P90 market size estimates + full distribution\n5. Identify top 3 swing variables by sensitivity analysis (Tornado chart)\n\n**Benchmark standard (consulting):** Top-down models should validate against orthogonal checks within ±15%. Sensitivity matrix should identify and document the top 3 assumptions driving >70% of variance.\n\n---\n\n### Method 6: Bayesian Sequential Updating\n\n**Use case:** Iteratively refining a market size estimate as new data arrives (pilot results, survey data, analogue market data).\n\n**Protocol:**\n1. Establish prior distribution for market size (from analogues, expert elicitation, or existing TAM ranges)\n2. Collect data (customer surveys, win rates, field interviews)\n3. Update posterior using Bayes' theorem\n4. Repeat at each data-collection milestone\n\n**Healthcare note:** Particularly useful for payer-side markets where ground-truth adoption data is scarce and available only through CMS claims, KLAS research, or proprietary databases.\n\n---\n\n### Method 7: Stanford Biodesign Dual Reconciliation (Healthcare-Specific)\n\n**Use case:** Sizing healthcare technology markets where clinical need and solution penetration diverge dramatically.\n\n**Protocol (from Stanford Biodesign curriculum):**\n1. Top-down: Size the clinical need (prevalence × treatment-seeking rate × average cost per patient)\n2. Bottom-up: Sum current solution revenues (existing products × users × annual cost)\n3. Accept the gap as a diagnostic: bottom-up = current market; top-down = addressable potential if superior solution achieves clinical penetration\n4. Derive market confidence interval from the spread between the two estimates\n5. Identify the assumption driving the gap (treatment-seeking rate, reimbursement, price sensitivity) — that assumption is the key risk\n\n---\n\n## Source Stack\n\n### Tier 1: University Academic Sources (Peer-Reviewed or Authored)\n\n**Harvard Business School**\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| Marketing Analysis Toolkit: Market Size and Market Share Analysis | Thomas J. (Abridged) | Clayton M. Christensen, Derek C. M. van Bever | HBS Faculty Research, December 2024 | https://hbs.edu/faculty/Pages/item.aspx?num=51553 |\n\n**HBS Methodological Contribution:**\n- Steenburgh & Avery (HBS Note 510-081) is the canonical HBS market sizing toolkit. It teaches market build-up methodology: measure total market demand, segment by customer type, calculate penetration rates and market share. Includes pre-built Excel models.\n- Christensen's JTBD reframes market definition — the relevant market is not the product category but the \"job\" the customer is trying to accomplish. This expands TAM by redefining market boundaries beyond incumbent product categories.\n\n---\n\n**MIT Sloan**\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| Pair People and AI for Better Product Demand Forecasting | Devadrita Nair, Maria Jesus Saenz | MIT Sloan Management Review, January 2024 | https://sloanreview.mit.edu/article/pair-people-and-ai-for-better-product-demand-forecasting/ |\n| Forecasting New Product Life Cycle Curves: Practical Approach and Empirical Analysis | K. Hu, J. Acimovic, F. Erize et al. | Manufacturing & Service Operations Management 21, no. 1 (Winter 2019): 66–85 | — |\n\n**MIT Sloan Methodological Contribution:**\n- MIT Digital Supply Chain Transformation Lab (Saenz) emphasizes hybrid human+AI demand forecasting for products with limited historical data and short life cycles — directly applicable to nascent health IT markets. Key finding: algorithmic forecasting improves accuracy but requires human contextualization for market-changing events.\n- MIT framework distinguishes short-lifecycle products (limited history, high fad risk) from durable adoption curves (more amenable to Bass-type modeling).\n\n---\n\n**Stanford Graduate School of Business / Stanford Biodesign**\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| Biodesign: The Process of Innovating Medical Technologies | Stefanos Zenios, Josh Makower, Paul Yock, et al. | Cambridge University Press, 2009 (2nd ed.) | https://gsb.stanford.edu/faculty-research/books/biodesign-process-innovating-medical-technologies |\n| Top-Down and Bottom-Up Market Sizing Example | Lyn Denend, Michelle de Haaff, Ravi Pamnani | Stanford Biodesign Toolkit V1, © 2022 Stanford University | https://biodesignguide.stanford.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Top-Down-and-Bottom-Up-Market-Sizing-Example.pdf |\n| 2.4 Market Analysis — Biodesign Process | Paul Yock, Stefanos Zenios, et al. | eBiodesign.org Chapter 2.4 (live resource) | https://ebiodesign.org/chapter/2-4-market-analysis/ |\n| Application of Stanford Biodesign Framework in Healthcare Innovation Training | Shefaly Shorey, et al. (scoping review, 26 articles) | Journal of Medical Systems 48, article 44 (2024). DOI: 10.1007/s10916-024-02067-x | https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/38647719/ |\n\n**Stanford Biodesign Methodological Contribution:**\n- The definitive healthcare-specific market sizing protocol: dual top-down/bottom-up with explicit reconciliation.\n- Step sequence: (1) landscape the total disease market, (2) segment by patient/treatment/provider/payer, (3) size each segment using both methods, (4) reconcile and document the assumption driving the gap, (5) apply Porter's Five Forces to validate competitive dynamics.\n- The \"gap diagnosis\" is a unique healthcare contribution: when top-down >> bottom-up, the excess represents unrealized need due to suboptimal existing solutions. This is the target market for disruptive health IT.\n- Data sources explicitly referenced: MEPS (AHRQ), PubMed, CMS pricing, FDA product labels.\n\n---\n\n**Wharton School, University of Pennsylvania**\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| A Conjoint Approach to Multipart Pricing | Raghuram Iyengar (Wharton), Kamel Jedidi, Rajeev Kohli | Journal of Marketing Research, Vol. XLV (April 2008): 195–210 | https://faculty.wharton.upenn.edu/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/Conjoint_pricing.pdf |\n| Demand Forecasting: Evidence-Based Methods and Checklists | J. Scott Armstrong (Wharton), Kesten Green | Working Paper (2017), Wharton School / University of South Australia | https://faculty.wharton.upenn.edu/wp-content/uploads/2017/05/JSA-Demand-Forecasting-89-clean.pdf |\n| Paul E. Green — \"Father of Conjoint Analysis\" | Paul E. Green (Wharton, retired 2005) | Profile: Wharton Magazine (2005, 2007) | https://magazine.wharton.upenn.edu/issues/spring-2005/whartons-paul-green-helping-companies-develop-products-consumers-actually-want/ |\n\n**Wharton Methodological Contribution:**\n- Paul E. Green (1927–2012) invented conjoint analysis in the 1960s while at Wharton. He introduced Bayesian analysis to marketing. Conjoint analysis is now the standard tool for demand estimation and willingness-to-pay research feeding market sizing. His framework is used by Marriott (Courtyard by Marriott design), EZPass, and pharmaceutical pricing globally.\n- Armstrong & Green (Wharton, 2017): The most comprehensive evidence-based survey of demand forecasting methods. Reviews 18 evidence-based methods. Key finding: structured quantitative methods outperform unaided judgment; combining multiple methods reduces forecast error by >50%. Conjoint analysis is explicitly listed as an evidence-based demand forecasting method.\n- Iyengar et al. (Wharton, 2008): Advanced conjoint for multipart pricing (subscription + usage fees) — directly applicable to SaaS and healthcare IT where pricing is bundled.\n\n---\n\n### Tier 2: Foundational Academic Papers (Peer-Reviewed)\n\n**Bass Diffusion Model (Core Source)**\n\n| Source | Author | Citation | URL |\n|--------|--------|----------|-----|\n| A New Product Growth Model for Consumer Durables | Frank M. Bass (Purdue University) | Management Science 15, no. 5 (January 1969): 215–227. DOI: 10.1287/mnsc.15.5.215 | https://pubsonline.informs.org/doi/abs/10.1287/mnsc.15.5.215 |\n| A New Product Growth Model for Consumer Durables (50th anniversary republication) | Frank M. Bass | Management Science 50, no. 12 supplement (December 2004): 1825–1832. DOI: 10.1287/mnsc.1040.0264 | https://ideas.repec.org/a/inm/ormnsc/v50y2004i12_supplementp1825-1832.html |\n| PDF (free access) | Frank M. Bass | University of Vermont repository | https://pdodds.w3.uvm.edu/files/papers/others/1969/bass1969a.pdf |\n\n**Bass Model Methodological Contribution:**\n- 5,145+ citations. The foundational equation for technology adoption forecasting. Empirically validated against 11 consumer durable categories. Yields good predictions of peak sales timing and magnitude.\n- Parameters: p (innovation/external influence, typically 0.01–0.05) and q (imitation/internal influence, typically 0.3–0.6). Market potential m is estimated from TAM analysis.\n- Appropriate when: a technology is in early diffusion, analogous historical adoption data exists, and the market has a defined ceiling.\n\n---\n\n**Rogers Diffusion of Innovations (Adoption Curve Theory)**\n\n| Source | Author | Citation | URL |\n|--------|--------|----------|-----|\n| Diffusion of Innovations (5th ed.) | Everett M. Rogers (USC Annenberg) | Free Press / Simon & Schuster, 2003 (first ed. 1962) | Standard library reference — PDF via: https://teddykw2.files.wordpress.com/2012/07/everett-m-rogers-diffusion-of-innovations.pdf |\n\n**Rogers Methodological Contribution:**\n- Defines five adopter categories: Innovators (2.5%), Early Adopters (13.5%), Early Majority (34%), Late Majority (34%), Laggards (16%).\n- Five innovation attributes that determine adoption speed: relative advantage, compatibility, complexity, trialability, observability.\n- The S-curve of cumulative adoption is the qualitative framework; Bass model provides the mathematical quantification.\n- Healthcare IT context: Rogers' framework is the dominant theoretical basis for EHR adoption research (Ford et al. 2006; Everson et al. 2018).\n\n---\n\n**Conjoint Analysis (Core Sources)**\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| Willingness-to-pay estimation with choice-based conjoint analysis | Gensler, Hinz, Skiera, Theysohn | European Journal of Operational Research, 2012. DOI: 10.1016/j.ejor.2012.01.002 | https://pure.rug.nl/ws/files/134690949/Willingness_to_pay_estimation_with_choice_based_conjoint_analysis_Addressing.pdf |\n\n---\n\n**Monte Carlo for Uncertainty Quantification**\n\n| Source | Author | Citation | URL |\n|--------|--------|----------|-----|\n| Modern Monte Carlo Methods for Efficient Uncertainty Quantification and Propagation: A Survey | Jiaxin Zhang (Oak Ridge National Laboratory) | arXiv:2011.00680 [stat.ME], November 2020 | https://arxiv.org/pdf/2011.00680 |\n\n**Monte Carlo Methodological Contribution:**\n- Comprehensive survey of Monte Carlo approaches for uncertainty quantification. Covers standard MC, Multilevel MC (MLMC), Multifidelity MC (MFMC), and variance reduction techniques.\n- Key practical finding: standard MC has convergence rate O(N^-1/2) — computationally intensive for tight confidence intervals. Latin hypercube sampling and quasi-Monte Carlo methods (Sobol sequences) significantly reduce required iterations.\n\n---\n\n**Bayesian Demand Estimation**\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| A Bayesian Approach to Demand Estimation | H. Kıvanc Aksoy, Asli Guner | Procedia Economics and Finance 26 (2015): 777–784. DOI: 10.1016/S2212-5671(15)00844-8 | https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2212567115008448 |\n| A Bayesian hierarchical modeling approach to combining multiple data sources: A case study in size estimation | Jacob Parsons, Xiaoyue Niu, Le Bao | arXiv:2012.05346 [stat.AP], 2020/2021 | https://arxiv.org/abs/2012.05346 |\n\n**Bayesian Methodological Contribution:**\n- Aksoy & Guner (2015): Demonstrates Bayesian framework for demand estimation using conjugate prior families. Prior = historical data or subjective expert belief. Posterior = updated estimate incorporating new observations. Applicable when historical data exists for analogous markets.\n- Parsons, Niu & Bao (2020): Bayesian hierarchical model for size estimation combining multiple data sources with conflicting estimates. Explicitly models systematic error in each data source. Directly applicable to healthcare market sizing where KLAS, IDC, and CMS data often conflict.\n\n---\n\n### Tier 3: Healthcare IT-Specific Academic Sources\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| Forecasting the Maturation of Electronic Health Record Functions Among US Hospitals: Retrospective Analysis and Predictive Model | Everson, et al. — Johns Hopkins Bloomberg School of Public Health / University of Washington | Journal of Medical Internet Research 2018;20(8):e10458. DOI: 10.2196/10458 | https://www.jmir.org/2018/8/e10458/ |\n| Predicting the Adoption of Electronic Health Records by Physicians: When Will Health Care be Paperless? | Eric W. Ford, Nir Menachemi, M. Thad Phillips | JAMIA 2006; PMC1380189 | https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC1380189/ |\n| Transforming the Healthcare Revenue Cycle with Artificial Intelligence in the USA | Oluwadamilola Adeleke, Simeon Ayo-Oluwa Ajayi (Rush University / U of Illinois) | International Journal of Multidisciplinary Research and Growth Evaluation, Vol. 5, Issue 6 (Nov–Dec 2024): 1670–1684 | https://www.allmultidisciplinaryjournal.com/uploads/archives/20250804155620_MGE-2025-4-080.1.pdf |\n| Hospital Trends in the Use, Evaluation, and Governance of Predictive AI, 2023–2024 | Chang, Owusu-Mensah, Everson, Richwine | ONC/ASTP Data Brief No. 80, September 2025 | https://healthit.gov/data/data-briefs/hospital-trends-use-evaluation-and-governance-predictive-ai-2023-2024/ |\n\n**Healthcare IT Methodological Contributions:**\n- Everson et al. (2018): Applied Bass diffusion model to HIMSS EMRAM hospital EHR adoption stages. Used 2006–2014 data. Achieved R² = 0.91. Forecasted Stage 7 (fully digital) not reached until 2035 absent policy intervention. This is the gold-standard published application of Bass diffusion to health IT.\n- Ford et al. (2006): Applied Bass diffusion to physician EHR adoption in small practices. Estimated p and q from six historical surveys. Generated optimistic, best-estimate, and pessimistic scenarios. Demonstrated that adoption in small practices lagged significantly behind projections.\n- ONC/ASTP Data Brief 80 (2025): 71% of U.S. hospitals use predictive AI integrated with EHR as of 2024, up from 66% in 2023. This is current market penetration data usable as an input to Bass or Rogers models for AI-embedded health IT sizing.\n\n---\n\n### Tier 4: Consulting Firm Published Frameworks\n\n**McKinsey Global Institute**\n\n| Source | Authors | Citation | URL |\n|--------|---------|----------|-----|\n| The Next Big Arenas of Competition | Michael Chui, Kevin Russell, Kweilin Ellingrud, et al. — McKinsey Global Institute | MGI Report, October 2024 | https://www.mckinsey.com/~/media/mckinsey/mckinsey%20global%20institute/our%20research/the-next-big-arenas-of-competition/the-next-big-arenas-of-competition_final.pdf |\n\n**McKinsey Methodological Contribution:**\n- MGI defines \"arenas\" as industries with both high growth and high competitive intensity (measured by market cap shuffle rate). Identifies 18 future arenas generating $29–$48 trillion by 2040.\n- The \"arena-creation potion\" framework: business model or technological step change + escalatory investment + large/growing TAM. This is MCK's published framework for identifying markets worth sizing.\n- McKinsey's market sizing interview methodology (McKinsey.com careers): 5-step structured approach — clarify, choose approach, build segmentation tree, calculate with round numbers, sanity check. Scores structure, judgment, and communication.\n\n**BCG / Bain (Consulting Standard)**\n\nThe consulting standard across McKinsey, BCG, and Bain for market sizing cases is the 5-step structured framework:\n1. Clarify the question (geography, time, units, inclusions/exclusions)\n2. Choose top-down or bottom-up (state reasoning)\n3. Build a MECE segmentation tree before calculating\n4. Calculate with round numbers, narrating assumptions explicitly\n5. Sanity-check against benchmarks (per-capita cross-check, competitor disclosed revenue)\n\nSource verification: McKinsey case interview prep (mckinsey.com/careers/interviewing), BCG case prep (careers.bcg.com), Bain case prep (bain.com/careers/interview-prep). Documented in Road to Offer (2026-03-15): https://www.roadtooffer.com/blog/market-sizing-framework\n\n**Umbrex Market Sizing Playbook (Ex-Consultant Standard)**\n\n| Source | URL |\n|--------|-----|\n| Foundations of Market Sizing | https://umbrex.com/resources/market-sizing-playbook/foundations-of-market-sizing/ |\n| Top-Down Market Sizing Methodology (12-step) | https://umbrex.com/resources/market-sizing-playbook/top-down-market-sizing-methodology/ |\n\n**Umbrex Key Standards:**\n- TAM must carry an explicit time stamp\n- Model must validate against orthogonal check within ±15%\n- Sensitivity matrix must identify top 3 swing assumptions\n- Growth drivers must be tied to causal levers, not extrapolated CAGR\n- Every input cell must have source link, reliability score, and last-updated date\n\n---\n\n## Key Experts (Recognized Authorities by Domain)\n\n| Expert | Affiliation | Domain | Key Work |\n|--------|-------------|--------|----------|\n| Thomas J. It is the reference document for market sizing pedagogy.\n\n2. **Stanford Biodesign has the only published dual-reconciliation protocol specifically for healthcare markets.** The reconciliation gap is not a problem to eliminate — it is diagnostic information. Bottom-up = current market; top-down = potential market if need is fully addressed.\n\n3. **The Bass diffusion model (1969) is the foundational quantitative tool for technology adoption forecasting.** With 5,145 citations and an empirically validated application to EHR adoption (R² = 0.91, Everson et al. 2018), it is the appropriate tool for sizing early-stage health IT markets with defined analogues. Parameters p and q for health IT are estimable from HIMSS, ONC, and AHA datasets.\n\n4. **Conjoint analysis is the standard tool for demand estimation when willingness to pay is uncertain.** Invented at Wharton (Paul E. Green), now standard across B2C and B2B. Choice-based conjoint (CBC) with Hierarchical Bayes estimation is the current gold standard.\n\n5. **The consulting standard for market sizing cases** (McKinsey, BCG, Bain) is the 5-step structured framework: clarify → choose approach → build MECE tree → calculate → sanity-check. Top-down is appropriate ~80% of the time; bottom-up is mandatory for B2B with countable accounts.\n\n6. **Monte Carlo simulation is the standard for expressing market sizing uncertainty.** P10/P50/P90 output is more defensible than a single-point estimate. The top 3 swing assumptions should be documented in a sensitivity (Tornado) chart.\n\n### What is unclear or debated\n\n1. **Bass model parameter estimation for nascent markets.** When no comparable technology exists, choosing analogous products for p and q benchmarking requires judgment. No consensus protocol exists for selecting analogues.\n\n2. **When does top-down significantly overstate versus accurately project?** The Stanford Biodesign case study showed a 10:1 gap ($2.1B top-down vs. $206M bottom-up) for a healthcare consumer product. This degree of divergence is typical for markets with high unmet need but low current treatment rates. The protocol for deciding which to use as the planning number is not universally agreed.\n\n3. **Bayesian priors in healthcare IT markets.** Where to anchor the prior distribution when a market is genuinely new (no historical analogue) is an open methodological question. Most practitioners use analogous technology markets with judgment adjustments.\n\n4. **Bottom-up accuracy for payer-side health IT markets.** Payer IT spending is not publicly disclosed with the same granularity as hospital IT (HIMSS, AHA). This creates a structural data gap for RCM and payer analytics market sizing.\n\n---\n\n## Open Unknowns\n\n1. A comprehensive academic study directly comparing top-down vs. bottom-up forecast accuracy for health IT markets (analogous to Armstrong's general forecasting accuracy research) does not appear to exist in published literature.\n\n2. The Rogers/Bass framework was developed for consumer and industrial durable goods. Its validity for enterprise software with multi-year contract cycles, switching costs, and institutional procurement has been adapted but not formally validated at scale for health IT.\n\n3. No published academic paper directly benchmarks Bass diffusion parameters (p, q) specifically for revenue cycle management (RCM) software adoption — the closest analogue is EHR adoption research.\n\n---\n\n## Recommended Next Research Steps\n\n1. **Obtain HIMSS EMRAM 2024 data** to update Everson et al. (2018) Bass model parameters for current EHR maturation trajectory — these are the best available p and q anchors for health IT.\n\n2. **Pull ONC/AHA IT Supplement data** (publicly available) to establish current penetration rates for specific health IT categories (RCM automation, payer analytics, prior authorization automation) — these are the m values for Bass modeling.\n\n3. **Identify 3–5 analogous technology adoption curves** for the specific health IT category being sized (e.g., cloud EHR, AI billing, automated prior auth) to bound Bass parameter estimates.\n\n4. **Acquire access to KLAS Research data** — the authoritative source for health IT vendor market share and customer satisfaction. KLAS is used by every major health IT analyst firm as the bottom-up market validation tool.\n\n5. **For any category requiring WTP validation:** design a Choice-Based Conjoint study targeting hospital CFOs and revenue cycle directors. Budget: 4–8 weeks, 150–300 respondents per segment for statistically stable part-worth utilities.\n\n---\n\n*Research compiled 2026-03-22. Sources verified at publication URLs. All academic citations are to original published sources. Consulting frameworks are derived from publicly available firm websites and practitioner-compiled resources.*\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":"tool_use","stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":20581,"cache_read_input_tokens":79523,"output_tokens":9095,"server_tool_use":{"web_search_requests":0,"web_fetch_requests":0},"service_tier":"standard","cache_creation":{"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":20581},"inference_geo":"","iterations":[],"speed":"standard"}},"requestId":"req_011CZHdcTDMsj3M4c6e8sY7R","type":"assistant","uuid":"1d78028e-a4fa-48db-a3f3-b0aa7e2bff73","timestamp":"2026-03-22T06:21:20.710Z","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/mikerodgers/Startup-Intelligence-OS","sessionId":"fe36a5fb-a2bc-4eb6-b6f8-8a2ba119fc51","version":"2.1.78","gitBranch":"main","slug":"federated-percolating-pnueli"}
